storage</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Electrochemical energy storage has the advantages of high energy density, long cycle period, and environmental friendliness. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> Although lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have always played a leading role in the fields of wearable electronic devices and new energy vehicles, the gigantic application market puts urgent pressure on the increasingly scarce lithium resources (20 ppm). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> Thanks to the abundant sodium resources (23 000 ppm) in the earth’s crust and physical and chemical properties very similar to lithium, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have progressively attracted much attention in recent years. Like LIBs, the final performance of SIB devices is also highly dependent on the main components, including the electrode materials, electrolyte, and the separator. The traditional graphite materials that are used as LIB anodes are not appropriate for sodium storage due to the larger ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) than that of Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 4 to 6">(4−6)</a> During the charging and discharging processes, the Na<sup>+</sup> transport is significantly hindered in the graphite layers with a distance of 3.35 Å. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> In addition, the repeated sodiation and desodiation critically damage the layered structure of the graphite anodes. It is therefore an imperious demand to design alternative anode materials for rapid sodium storage with long-term stability.</div><div class="NLM_p">The state-of-art anode materials for SIBs include hard carbon, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> alloys, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref9" aria-label="reference 4 and 9">(4,9)</a> and metal compounds. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> Among them, molybdenum disulfide (MoS<sub>2</sub>) anodes have considerable development prospects on account of their graphite-like layered structure, large interlayer spacing (6.2 Å), and high theoretical capacity (672 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> Nevertheless, the intrinsic limitations of MoS<sub>2</sub> anodes arising from the easy agglomeration during use and poor electrical conductivity lead to a decrease in capacity under long-term cycling and low capacities at high current densities. To solve the issues, different strategies have been proposed, such as the synthesis of nanoscale MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes with controlled morphology and assembly, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 12 to 14">(12−14)</a> chemical composition, physical mixing or chemical interaction with conductive substrates (MXenes, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 15 to 17">(15−17)</a> graphene, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 18 to 20">(18−20)</a> carbon nanotubes, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 21 to 23">(21−23)</a> carbon spheres, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> etc.), and creating defect structures (S vacancies, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> Mo vacancies, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> and heteroatom doping <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 27 and 28">(27,28)</a>). In these ways, the electrode volume change during cycling can be well accommodated, or the electrical conductivity is improved, thus leading to an enhanced sodium storage performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">As a typical polymorphic material, MoS<sub>2</sub> possesses different crystal structures, i.e., the thermodynamically stable semiconductor phase (2H, S-MoS<sub>2</sub>), metastable metallic phase (1T, M-MoS<sub>2</sub>), and 3R phase. Among them, the intrinsic electrical conductivity of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase is ∼5 orders of magnitude higher than that of the S-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase, making it possible to further improve the ion storage of MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Currently, different M-MoS<sub>2</sub> nanostructures have been designed and dispersed on the outer surface of the carbon matrix to avoid the agglomeration induced by the van der Waals forces. But, the M-MoS<sub>2</sub> layers still peel off from the substrates due to the weak interactions. Although the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>–substrate adhesion can be improved by surface coating and then calcination at high temperatures, the metastable M-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase transforms into a stable S-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase in this process, which limits the applications of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes. Recently, covalent interactions between MoS<sub>2</sub> (S-MoS<sub>2</sub> and M-MoS<sub>2</sub>) anodes and carbon substrates have been introduced under mild conditions (hydrothermal, solvothermal, in situ assembly, ball milling, etc.), and their effects on the rapid and stable ion storage have also been demonstrated. Zhang et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> established Mo–N covalent bonds at the interface between sulfur-doped graphitic carbon nitride (SCN) and MoS<sub>2</sub>, which effectively promoted the shift of photogenerated carriers between SCN and MoS<sub>2</sub> and enhanced the photocatalytic efficiency and structural stability. Svetlana et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> introduced Mo–C covalent bonds to anchor MoS<sub>2</sub> at the hole boundaries of the holey graphene, which increased the electron transfer rate and the storage stability of Li<sup>+</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, we designed a novel M-MoS<sub>2</sub>-based composite electrode, which grew on the inner surface of hollow mesoporous carbon spheres (denoted M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS) by engineering the nanoscale morphology and surface structure of the carbon substrate. Experimentally, the electrode was synthesized by a simple template-assisted solvothermal reaction. Microstructure studies showed that the ultrathin M-MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets with a layer spacing of 0.7 nm grew on the inner surface of mesoporous HCS through a strong covalent bond. Thanks to the structural design, the anode materials demonstrated superior sodium storage performance, especially stable and high capacity, at various current densities. For example, the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode delivered a reversible capacity of 291.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. After 100 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the reversible capacity was 401.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a capacity retention rate of 79%. After 2500 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the electrode still had a reversible capacity of 320.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a capacity retention ratio of 75%. The current structural design strategy can also be used to prepare other transition-metal dichalcogenide-based electrodes for a wide range of applications.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Synthesis of HCS</h3><div class="NLM_p last">HCS were prepared by templated-assisted coprecipitation according to a previous study. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> First, an ammonium hydroxide solution (NH<sub>3</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O, 25%, 12 mL) was added to a mixed solution of ultrapure water (40 mL) and absolute ethanol (280 mL) under vigorous stirring. A tetraethyl orthosilicate (TEOS, 8.3 g) solution was added dropwise to the solution under stirring for 30 min. Subsequently, formaldehyde (F, 37 wt %, 2.24 mL) and resorcinol (R, 1.6 g) were added and stirred at room temperature for 24 h to obtain a brown precipitate of SiO<sub>2</sub>@SiO<sub>2</sub>/RF. The product was dried at 60 °C for 12 h and calcined at 800 °C for 2 h under the protection of an Ar atmosphere to form SiO<sub>2</sub>@SiO<sub>2</sub>/C. Finally, HCS were obtained using HF (10 wt %) washing to get rid of SiO<sub>2</sub>.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Synthesis of M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS, S-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS, and MoS<sub>2</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p last">MoO<sub>3</sub> (250 mg), thioacetamide (500 mg), and urea (500 mg) were added to a mixed solution of ultrapure water (30 mL) and absolute ethanol (30 mL) and then ultrasonicated for 0.5 h. Then, HCS (62.5 mg) were dispersed in the solution and sonicated for 1 h. The obtained black solution was transferred to a Teflon-lined stainless-steel autoclave (100 mL in volume) and kept at 200 °C for 24 h. After the solvothermal reaction, the sample was washed with ultrapure water and absolute ethanol and then dried at 60 °C under vacuum for 12 h to obtain M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS. The synthesis of a pure MoS<sub>2</sub> sample was the same as M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS but without the addition of HCS. To prepare S-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS, the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS sample was calcinated under an Ar atmosphere at 800 °C for 2 h with a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Synthesis of HCS@M-MoS<sub>2</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p last">MoO<sub>3</sub> (250 mg), thioacetamide (500 mg), and urea (500 mg) were dissolved in a mixed solution of ultrapure water (30 mL) and absolute ethanol (30 mL) and then sonicated for 0.5 h to achieve a well-dispersed solution. Then, SiO<sub>2</sub>@SiO<sub>2</sub>/C (62.5 mg) was added to the homogeneous solution and subjected to sonication for 1 h. Subsequently, the obtained black solution was transferred to a Teflon-lined stainless-steel autoclave (100 mL) and kept at 200 °C for 24 h. After that, the precipitation was washed with ultrapure water and absolute ethanol and dried at 60 °C under vacuum for 12 h. The resulting product was treated with a HF solution (10 wt %) to remove SiO<sub>2</sub> to obtain the HCS@M-MoS<sub>2</sub> sample.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM, Zeiss SUPRA 55 Sapphire, 5 kV) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEOL JEM-2100 F, 200 kV) were carried out to observe the internal and external surface morphology and microstructure of the samples. Phase identification, content analysis, and elemental composition analysis of samples were performed by X-ray diffraction (XRD, Rigaku Corporation SmartLab 9, Cu Kα irradiation), Raman spectroscopy (LabRAM ARAMIS), and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo ESCALAB 250XI, 532 nm laser excitation), respectively. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA, SETSYS Evolution) was performed in an air atmosphere with a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. N<sub>2</sub> absorption and desorption isotherms were obtained using an SSA-4000 instrument to study the specific surface area and pore size distribution of the samples.</div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical performance test was realized by assembling the CR2032 half-cell model. To fabricate the working electrodes, the active material, acetylene black, and PVDF were mixed at a ratio of 8:1:1. The mixture was then diluted with an appropriate amount of <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidinone (NMP) and evenly coated on a copper foil, which was cut into a circular pole piece with a diameter of 10 mm as the working electrode. The average mass loading of the active materials was 0.18 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. Both the counter electrode and the reference electrode were sodium foils. Glass microfiber was used as the separator, and the electrolyte was 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> diluted in a mixture of ethylene carbonate (EC)-dimethyl carbonate (DMC) (1:1 in volume), along with fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as an additive. A LAND CT2001A battery test system was employed to record electrochemical data. A CHI1000 electrochemical workstation was used to measure cyclic voltammograms (CV) ranging from 0.01 to 3.0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. Electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) were achieved using an EnergyLab XM electrochemical workstation with a 5 mV AC signal under a frequency ranging from 100 kHz to 10 mHz.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Composition, Microstructure, and Morphology</h3><div class="NLM_p">The synthesis process of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS sample is schematically illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>. The HCS sample was prepared by templated-assisted coprecipitation, calcination at a high temperature, and template removal. First, SiO<sub>2</sub> nanosphere templates were produced by TEOS hydrolysis in an alkaline environment, and then formaldehyde (F) and resorcinol (R) were polymerized on the template surface to form an organic layer (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a,b</a>). The organic coating was transferred into carbon by calcination at 800 °C in an Ar atmosphere (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1c,d</a>). The HCS sample was obtained by chemical etching of the SiO<sub>2</sub> template with HF solution. Before the solvothermal reaction, the precursors (including MoO<sub>3</sub>, thioacetamide, and urea) and HCS mixture were subjected to ultrasonication. In this process, CH<sub>3</sub>CSNH<sub>2</sub> molecules decomposed and generated hydrogen sulfide (H<sub>2</sub>S) gas, which was preferentially absorbed on the rough inner surface of HCS and captured inside due to space confinement. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> In the subsequent solvothermal procedure, the molybdenum source and urea diffused into the cavity of HCS through the surface mesopores and reacted with H<sub>2</sub>S gas to form MoS<sub>2</sub> nanostructures, finally resulting in the production of MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS composites. The M-MoS<sub>2</sub> formation is considered due to the synergy of urea and CH<sub>3</sub>CSNH<sub>2</sub> molecules. In addition to serving as the sulfur source, the CH<sub>3</sub>CSNH<sub>2</sub> molecule added generates an acidic environment (pH ≈ 4) in which the octahedral structure of MoO<sub>3</sub> (molybdenum source) can be stably maintained. The resulting amorphous cluster MoS<sub>3</sub> was then gradually reduced by urea, and a single layer of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> was gradually formed as the MoS<sub>3</sub> crystal cluster grew. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> The formation of MoS<sub>2</sub> is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a>.<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/medium/am1c18269_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i11"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a, the HCS synthesized by the extended Stöber method <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> exhibit a regular shape, smooth surface, and uniform size with an average diameter of ∼200 nm and is uniformly dispersed without significant agglomeration. After the solvothermal treatment, the spherelike morphology was preserved. Although the surface becomes a little coarse, no obvious growth of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets, which is the character of typical MoS<sub>2</sub> nanostructures (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a,b</a>), can be observed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b). The coarse surface is due to the extended growth of a few MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets from the inside to the outside through the carbon shells (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c,d). The observations clearly demonstrate that most of the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets were confined in the HCS cavities. In addition, it is found that the average thickness of HCS is ∼13 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d), and mesopores with a mean diameter of ∼5 nm can also be found on the HCS surface (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d1). The thin carbon shell together with the surface mesopores is favorable for the mass diffusion into the cavity to promote the formation of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> inside the HCS. Meanwhile, such a microstructure character also facilitates the transport of electrons and ions during the sodiation/desodiation process. As seen in the HRTEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d2), the interplanar distances of ∼0.7 and ∼0.27 nm can be measured, which correspond to the (002) and (100) crystalline planes of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase, respectively. The dotlike disordered contrast around the M-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase is ascribed to amorphous carbon, which is also confirmed by the Raman spectrum. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a> shows a typical Raman spectrum of HCS templates. The spectrum contains two characteristic peaks appearing at ∼1353 and 1595 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which correspond to the carbon bands D and G, respectively. Generally, the intensity ratio of the two bands (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) is used to evaluate the disorder degree in carbon materials. A larger <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value indicates a higher disorder degree and more defects. Previous studies demonstrated that the disordered structure facilitates the rapid transportation of Na<sup>+</sup>, thus exhibiting higher rate performance and higher specific capacity than graphite carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a> The calculated intensity ratio of the D band to the G band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) is close to 1, demonstrating the low crystallinity and presence of structural disorder and rich defects in HCS. The disordered structures of the HCS template provide heterogeneous nucleation sites for MoS<sub>2</sub> formation and strengthen the interactions between the growing material and the carbon substrate via possible covalent interactions, as will be discussed below. In the selected area electron diffraction pattern (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e), no distinct diffraction rings or points can be noted, accounting for the low crystallinity of the sample. High-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) image and the energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mapping results are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f. The Mo and S elements are mainly distributed inside the HCS, and no noticeable signals can be detected outside the HCS template, further demonstrating the successful construction of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> nanostructures inside the HCS.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/medium/am1c18269_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic illustration of the synthesis process of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS sample.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c18269&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/medium/am1c18269_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. SEM images of (a) HCS and (b) M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS. (c, d) TEM and HRTEM images, (e) SAED pattern, and (f) HAADF-STEM image and EDS mapping of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS sample.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c18269&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The surface morphology of other samples is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>. When the added HCS amount is small (25, 37.5, and 50 mg), the limited internal space cannot accommodate the entire growth of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> inside the HCS. Growth on the outer surface can thus be observed (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a–c</a>) by increasing the HCS amount (62.5 and 70 mg) and more internal space is available for M-MoS<sub>2</sub> growth. Therefore, MoS<sub>2</sub> mainly grew inside the HCS, while the HCS surface was smooth with few additional growths (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4d,e</a>). Without the addition of HCS, pure M-MoS<sub>2</sub> samples can be obtained (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a,b</a>). The product shows a spherelike morphology, which is assembled by MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets. The result is similar to that reported in a previous study. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> The obtained M-MoS<sub>2</sub> in the composites can be converted into the semiconductor phase (S-MoS<sub>2</sub>) by a simple calcining treatment at 800 °C. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2c,d</a>, the obtained S-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS product possesses a morphology similar to that of the initial M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS sample. As a control, the M-MoS<sub>2</sub> sample was also grown on the outer surface of HCS (HCS@M-MoS2). During the solvothermal treatment, HCS was replaced by SiO<sub>2</sub>@SiO<sub>2</sub>/C, and the SiO<sub>2</sub> template was removed after the growth of MoS<sub>2</sub>. In this way, the MoS<sub>2</sub> sample can only load on the outside of the HCS (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2e,f</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a represents the crystalline structures of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> and S-MoS<sub>2</sub> polytypes. In both M-MoS<sub>2</sub> and S-MoS<sub>2</sub> lattices, the Mo atoms are linked by the surrounding six S atoms to form sandwichlike S–Mo–S layers, and the Mo atoms occupy the center of the sandwiched layers. The difference in the two lattices lies in the coordination geometry of the Mo and S atoms. In the M-MoS<sub>2</sub> structure, the coordination of Mo with S atoms is octahedral, and the S atoms are in the upper and lower planes. However, for the S-MoS<sub>2</sub> structure, the Mo atoms are in the trigonal prismatic arrangement constituted by S atoms. The phase composition and crystalline structure of the samples were experimentally identified by XRD studies (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b). For the S-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS sample, the diffraction peaks located at 2θ = 14.19, 33.08, 39.26, 49.04, 58.5, and 68.7° are derived from the (002), (100), (103), (105), (110), and (114) crystal planes of the S-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase with a hexagonal structure (JCPDS No. 75-1539). For the M-MoS2@HCS sample, the peaks at 2θ = 10.32 and 19.9° originate from the (002) and (004) crystal planes of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> Compared with S-MoS<sub>2</sub>, the (002) peak of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> shifts noticeably toward a lower diffraction angle, indicating that the M-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase has a larger (002) crystal plane spacing, which is consistent with the HRTEM measurements (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d2). The broad humps centered around 22 and 44° further confirm the amorphous structure of the carbon matrix (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). The XRD results of the HCS@M-MoS<sub>2</sub> composite and the pure M-MoS<sub>2</sub> sample are basically the same as M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS, also implying the phase purity of the samples. The specific symmetry of S atoms between M-MoS<sub>2</sub> and S-MoS<sub>2</sub> brought a noticeable difference in Raman vibration modes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c). In the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS composite, the Raman bands at 143.5 cm<sup>–1</sup> (J<sub>1</sub>), 233.7 cm<sup>–1</sup> (J<sub>2</sub>), 280.3 cm<sup>–1</sup> (E<sub>1g</sub>), and 333.2 cm<sup>–1</sup> (J<sub>3</sub>) are derived from the Mo–Mo stretching vibration of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> With the S-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS composite, the peaks at 376.59 cm<sup>–1</sup> (E<sub>2g</sub><sup>1</sup>) and 405.23 cm<sup>–1</sup> (A<sub>1g</sub>) are due to the in-plane and out-of-plane Mo-S modes of S-MoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> In addition, the appearance of weak E<sub>2g</sub><sup>1</sup> and A<sub>1g</sub> bands in the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS composite suggests a small amount of S-MoS<sub>2</sub> also formed in the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS composite. Further analysis shows that the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> values of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS and S-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS samples are both close to 1, indicating that the composite formation does not affect the defect structure of the HCS substrate.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/medium/am1c18269_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Structural representation of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> and S-MoS<sub>2</sub> polytypes. (b) XRD patterns and (c) Raman spectra of M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS and S-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS. (d) TGA curves of pure M-MoS<sub>2</sub> and M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS. (e) BET analysis and pore size distributions (inset) of HCS and M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c18269&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">XPS was employed to analyze the element valence state and M-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase ratio in the composite. The XPS survey spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a) shows the presence of Mo, S, C, and O elements. The two main peaks that emerged at 284.8 and 285.7 eV are assigned to C–C and C–O of C 1s (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b). Two typical peaks at 232.2 eV (Mo 3d<sub>3/2</sub>) and 229.1 eV (Mo 3d<sub>5/2</sub>) appear from the M-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c). By deconvolving the characteristic peak areas of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> and S-MoS<sub>2</sub>, the proportion of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase in the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS composite is calculated to be ∼52.9%. Similar results can be obtained from the high-resolution spectrum of S 2p (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d). The peaks at 226.5 and 235.9 eV correspond to S 2s and the Mo–O–C covalent bond, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 38 and 39">(38,39)</a> respectively. The covalent bond formation indicates the interface interaction between M-MoS<sub>2</sub> and HCS, which may arise from the surface structure of HCS. First, during the preparation of SiO<sub>2</sub>@SiO<sub>2</sub>@C, the carbon inner surface is protected by SiO<sub>2</sub> and retains hydrophilicity (with oxygen-containing surface functional groups) after HF etching treatment. This is the key factor in the formation of a Mo–O–C covalent bond. Second, the rough inner surface with rich defects of HCS provides abundant chemical anchoring sites for M-MoS<sub>2</sub> nucleation and growth. With such interface covalent interactions, the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets are pinned to the HCS inner surface to strengthen morphology stability. Furthermore, the strong chemical interactions induce electron excursion and enhance the electron transfer rate. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> From the high-resolution XPS spectrum of M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c,d), it can be seen that the binding energies of Mo 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Mo 3d<sub>5/2</sub> of M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS are 232.3 and 229.1 eV, respectively, compared with the M-MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets (231.8 and 228.7 eV, respectively) prepared under the same conditions without the carbon matrix, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> and they have shifted a high binding energy. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> The S 2P<sub>3/2</sub> (162.05 eV) and S 2P<sub>1/2</sub> (163 eV) of MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS also show the same result when compared with S 2P<sub>3/2</sub> (161.6 eV) and S 2P<sub>1/2</sub> (162.7 eV) of M-MoS<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/medium/am1c18269_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) XPS survey spectrum and high-resolution spectra of (b) C 1s, (c) Mo 3d, and (d) S 2p of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS sample.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c18269&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The carbon content in the MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS sample was studied by thermal gravity analysis (TGA), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d. After calcination from the room temperature to 600 °C in air flow (heating rate 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup>), the sample mass decreases in different stages. The mass loss below ∼200 °C is on account of the evaporation of free water. The further mass loss includes the conversion of MoS<sub>2</sub> to MoO<sub>3</sub> and the reaction of carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 41 and 42">(41,42)</a> From the analysis, the mass fraction of the MoS<sub>2</sub> phase in the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS composite is 51.9% calculated by the equation <i>m</i> × (1–35%) = <i>m</i> × <i>x</i> × (1–67.4%) + <i>m</i> × (1 – <i>x</i>), where <i>m</i> and <i>x</i> represent the mass of M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS and the mass ratio of MoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively. The specific surface area of the samples was measured by the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method, and the pore size distribution was recorded by the Barrett–Joyner–Halenda model (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e). The BET surface area of HCS is up to 661.9 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. After the growth of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> inside, the surface area decreases to 225.4 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. Nevertheless, the surface area of the MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS sample is still larger than those of the typical MoS<sub>2</sub> and carbon-based composites, such as MoS<sub>2</sub>@HPCS (77.5 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> CAHCSs@1T/2H–MoS<sub>2</sub> (30.6 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> BMCNs@MoS<sub>2</sub>-300 (28.76 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> codoped 1T-MoS2@N, S-doped carbon (134 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> MoS<sub>2</sub>/C CSs (12 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> and MoS<sub>2</sub>@C (9.1 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> Moreover, the pore size analysis (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e inset) shows that the pore diameters of the two samples are around 5 nm, which is in agreement with the TEM results (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d1).</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> Sodium Storage Properties</h3><div class="NLM_p">CV measurements were performed to analyze the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of the different electrodes. The initial three CV curves recorded at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> for the M-MoS2@HCS electrode are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a. A broad cathode peak at 0.7–1.0 V is observed in the first cycle and then disappears in the subsequent cycles. This peak results from the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film and the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into the MoS<sub>2</sub> lattice (MoS<sub>2</sub> + <i>x</i>Na<sup>+</sup> + <i>x</i>e<sup>–</sup> → Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> The sharp cathode peak at 0.05 V is attributed to the transformation of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> into Na<sub>2</sub>S (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> + (4 – <i>x</i>)Na<sup>+</sup> + (4 – <i>x</i>)e<sup>–</sup> → Mo + 2Na<sub>2</sub>S). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> In the first anodic scan, the peak at 1.63 V is attributed to the oxidation of Mo to form MoS<sub>2.</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g illustrates the initial intercalation and conversion reactions that occurred in the discharge process. The pure M-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b) and HCS@M-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a</a>) have CV curves similar to the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode. In contrast, there is a noticeable peak at 0.99 V in the first cathodic scan process for the S-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c). This evident reduction peak is attributed to the semiconductor-to-metal phase transition in the sodiated MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>h). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> The reduction peak at 0.34 V is due to the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mo from the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> phase through the conversion reaction, and the reduction peak at 0.61 V, which disappears in the subsequent cycle, is ascribed to the SEI film generation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref26" aria-label="reference 14 and 26">(14,26)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d demonstrates the charge/discharge profiles of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode with different cycles at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. In the first discharge curve, a voltage plateau can be seen at ∼1.0 V. It originates from the Na<sup>+</sup> inserted into the MoS<sub>2</sub> lattice and carbon matrix, which is in accordance with the CV results (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6b</a>). The initial discharge and charge capacities are 1467.9 and 508.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, yielding a low initial Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 34.6%. A large irreversible capacity loss is normally observed in typical conversion-based anodes, and it mainly comes from the SEI film formation and the electrolyte decomposition. In addition, the low electrical and/or ionic conductivity of the product yielded during the sodiation/desodiation process may also cause an irreversible capacity loss. The following charge/discharge curves are highly coincident, and there is no visible deviation until the 75th cycle, indicating the good stability of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode. For the pure M-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode, the platform at 1.7 V in the first charging curve is due to the conversion of Mo to MoS<sub>2</sub>. The platform below 0.3 V originates due to the transition of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> to Na<sub>2</sub>S, which also agrees with the CV results. Both the charge/discharge curves of HCS@M-MoS<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6c</a>) and HCS (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6d</a>) electrodes show platforms at ∼1.0 V, which is not observed for the pure M-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e), demonstrating the absence of a carbon matrix in the pure M-MoS<sub>2</sub> sample. The plateau at ∼0.9 V in the initial discharge curve of the S-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode attributed to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> induced the S-MoS<sub>2</sub> to M-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase transition (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f). The better electrochemical performance of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode is due to the crystalline and phase structures. Compared with S-MoS<sub>2</sub>, the M-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase has a larger interlayer spacing and better dispersibility, and therefore, it shows higher reversible capacity. Second, experimental and theoretical studies suggest that the releasing of lattice S atoms during the discharging process is the main reason for the decline in the capacity in the S-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode, while such release of S atoms can be effectively inhibited in the M-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> In addition, less phase transformation during the sodiation process is another reason for the better stability of M-MoS<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> During the sodiation process, S-MoS<sub>2</sub> is first transformed into M-MoS<sub>2</sub> and then into Na<sub>2</sub>S, while M-MoS<sub>2</sub> is directly transformed into Na<sub>2</sub>S. More phase transitions may hinder the effective transformation of the structure, resulting in structural damage and capacity degradation. Similar results are also obtained in tin sulfide electrodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/medium/am1c18269_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. CV curves of (a) M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS, (b) pure M-MoS<sub>2</sub>, and (c) S-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS over a voltage window of 0.01–3.0 V at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. Charge/discharge profiles of (d) M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS, (e) pure M-MoS<sub>2</sub>, and (f) S-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (g) Schematic diagram of Na<sup>+</sup> storage process of M-MoS<sub>2</sub>. (h) Schematic diagram of structure transformation during Na<sup>+</sup> storage of S-MoS<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c18269&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The rate capability of different electrodes measured at current densities ranging from 0.1 to 5.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> is displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a. The HCS electrode possesses a stable performance but with a low capacity, for example, at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. In comparison, the pure M-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode has a higher capacity but it cannot be retained in subsequent cycles. By growing M-MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets inside the HCS, a stable structure that inherits the advantages of both can be constructed. The resulting M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode not only has a high reversible capacity but also holds excellent stability. The reversible capacities at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> are 428.8, 396.8, 374.9, 361.8, and 343.22 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Even at a current density of 5.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the composite electrode still preserves a high capacity of 291.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. By changing the current density back to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the reversible capacity also increases to 462.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. In comparison to the reported typical MoS<sub>2</sub>-based electrodes for sodium storage, the performance of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS sample is also remarkable, especially at current densities higher than 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b). Moreover, the MoS<sub>2</sub>-based electrode also maintains high capacities under long-term operations, i.e., the composite shows good cycling stability. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c, at a low current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the reversible capacities of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode are 508.1 and 401.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after the first cycle and the 100th cycle, and the capacity retention rate is 79%. In contrast, S-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS, HCS, and pure M-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes have lower reversible capacities of 273.7, 129.9, and 68.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a</a>), which are only 68.2, 32.4, and 17.0% of the reversible capacity of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode. At a high current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the performance of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode is still satisfactory (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d). After the precirculation pretreatment at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> during the first three cycles, the reversible capacity of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode is 428.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. After 2500 cycles of long-term operation, the reversible capacity is 320.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to a capacity retention rate of 75%. In addition, the CE value is close to 100%. Under the same test conditions and after 500 cycles, the reversible capacities of S-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS, HCS, HCS@M-MoS<sub>2</sub>, and pure M-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes are only 115.2, 122.2, 75.2, and 48.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7b</a>), far below that of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode. The M-MoS<sub>2</sub>-to-HCS mass ratio in the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS composite also affects the sodium storage performance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). In the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS-75 electrode, the reversible capacity is low due to the low MoS<sub>2</sub> quality (25%) as the main active material. But if the M-MoS<sub>2</sub> mass ratio is too high, for example, 50% for the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS-50 electrode, then the M-MoS<sub>2</sub> growth is not only restricted inside the HCS but also on the outer surface of the HCS (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). The overgrowth of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> layers on the HCS surface blocks the mesopores on the carbon shells, leading to sluggish ion and mass transportation and decreased active sites for sodium storage. As a result, the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS-50 electrode shows low capacity and poor rate capability. The sample (M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode) with the best electrochemical performance includes 37.5 wt % M-MoS<sub>2</sub> and 62.5 wt % HCS-50. The electrochemical performance of the optimized sample is in contrast to the recently reported MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anode materials, as listed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. It is clear that the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode has sodium storage performance, especially the rate capability (291.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>@5A g<sup>–1</sup>), superior to the other MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes, such as BD-MoS<sub>2</sub> (262 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>@5A g<sup>–1</sup>), MoS<sub>2</sub>/GNs-AG (212.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>@5A g<sup>–1</sup>), MoS<sub>2</sub>-PPy (200 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>@5A g<sup>–1</sup>), and MoS<sub>2</sub>@CNTs (258 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>@5A g<sup>–1</sup>).</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/medium/am1c18269_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Rate capability of HCS, pure M-MoS<sub>2</sub>, and M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrodes. (b) Comparison of the rate capability of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode with the typical MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anodes for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56 ref57 ref58 ref59 ref60 ref61 ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53 ref54 ref55 ref56 ref57 ref58 ref59 ref60 ref61 ref62" aria-label="reference 53 to 62">(53−62)</a> Cycling performance of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode at current densities of (c) 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and (d) 1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c18269&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To analyze the source of the superior rate capability and ion storage mechanism of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode, we measured the CV response at different scan rates (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a). Generally, the peak current (<i>i</i>, mA) in the CV curves and the scan rate (<i>v</i>, mV s<sup>–1</sup>) satisfy the following power relation <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref63 ref64'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref63 ref64" aria-label="reference 63 and 64">(63,64)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/medium/am1c18269_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i13"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are constants. For the redox reaction, semi-infinite diffusion dominates the process with a <i>b</i> value of 0.5 (battery-like behavior); if the current <i>i</i> changes linearly with <i>v</i> (<i>b</i> = 1), then the reaction is a surface-controlled process (capacitive behavior). By linear fitting the plot of log(<i>i</i>) vs log(<i>v</i>), the <i>b</i> value can be extracted. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b, the <i>b</i> values calculated from the oxidation peak (0.99) and the reduction peak (0.88) are close to 1, indicating that the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode has a typical capacitive behavior for sodium storage.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/medium/am1c18269_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) CV curves of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode at various scan rates. (b) Plots of log(<i>i</i>) vs log(<i>v</i>) and their fitting lines (<i>i</i> is the peak current, and <i>v</i> is the scan rate). (c) Contribution ratio of capacitive and ion diffusion to the total capacity at various scan rates. (d) CV profile with the capacitive contribution at a scan rate of 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c18269&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The contribution percentage of capacitive behavior at a specific scan rate can be quantified using <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq 3</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/medium/am1c18269_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i14"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span>where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> represent the capacity contributed by battery-like behavior and capacitive behavior, respectively. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>c presents the contribution ratio of capacitive and ion diffusion to the total capacity of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode. At 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the capacitive contribution ratio is 55.2%. The contribution ratio of the capacitance enhances with the scan rate, reaching 88.3% at 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>d). The prevailing capacitance contribution especially at a high scan rate relates to the good rate capability of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode. The capacitive behavior is possibly due to the high specific surface area of the composite and the layered structure of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> with an expanded interlayer distance (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">The ion kinetics of different electrodes were studied by EIS, and the Nyquist plots measured after one cycle at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>. A representative plot includes an inclined line at a low frequency, which corresponds to the ion diffusion inside the electrode, and a semicircle at the medium frequency, which relates to the charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) between the electrode surface and electrolyte. According to the equivalent electrical circuit (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a> inset), the kinetic parameters can be obtained (<a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>) by fitting the measured data points. Thanks to the high electrical conductivity of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values for HCS@M-MoS<sub>2</sub> (214.7 Ω) and M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS (234 Ω) electrodes are much lower than those of S-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS (349.4 Ω) and HCS (552.6 Ω) electrodes. The slightly higher <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> for M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS than that of HCS@M-MoS<sub>2</sub> is due to the growth of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> inside HCS.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/medium/am1c18269_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0009.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Nyquist plots of M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS, S-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS, HCS@M-MoS<sub>2</sub>, and HCS electrodes after one cycle at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The inset is the equivalent electrical circuit used for curve fitting and obtaining the kinetic parameters, including <i>R</i><sub>s</sub> (solution resistance), <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> (charge-transfer resistance), <i>Z</i><sub>w</sub> (Warburg impedance), and CPE (constant phase-angle element).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c18269&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1. Fitting Parameters of M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS, S-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS, HCS@M-MoS<sub>2</sub>, and HCS Electrodes</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">electrodes</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>&nbsp;(Ω)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>R</i><sub>s</sub>&nbsp;(Ω)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">234</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.145</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">S-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">349.4</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">4.009</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HCS</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">552.6</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">3.333</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">HCS@M-MoS<sub>2</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">214.7</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">4.198</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">To analyze the sodium storage mechanism, we performed ex situ XRD measurements for the pure M-MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes. During the initial discharge process, the (002) peak of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> gradually becomes wide and shifts to a low angle, indicating the increase of the (002) layer spacing due to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>. With discharging to a low voltage area (below 0.3 V), a characteristic peak of the Na<sub>2</sub>S phase at 2θ = 12.9° starts to appear, which means that the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> phase transforms into Na<sub>2</sub>S. The result is consistent with the previous studies. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51 ref65'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51 ref65" aria-label="reference 51 and 65">(51,65)</a> In the subsequent charging process, the intensity of the Na<sub>2</sub>S characteristic peak decreases, which correlates to the MoS<sub>2</sub> phase. During the whole process, no characteristic peak of the S-MoS<sub>2</sub> phase appears. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref66'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref66" aria-label="reference 66">(66)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Combining the above structural characterization with the electrochemical measurements, we conclude that the unique structural design of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode leads to durable and rapid sodium storage with improved capacity. (1) <i>Enhanced stability</i>. The dispersion of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets on HCS can avoid the possible agglomeration induced by the van der Waals force between MoS<sub>2</sub> layers. Furthermore, the strong covalent interaction between M-MoS<sub>2</sub> and HCS substrate prevents the possible peeling off of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> layers from the substrates during the continuous cycling. In the worst case, if there is any peeling off occurring, those active materials can still be collected in HCS due to the internal growth nature. Finally, the ultrathin M-MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheet and HCS are flexible and thus can accommodate the volume expansion during the sodiation and desodiation cycling. Those merits contribute to the excellent cycling stability of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode. As a further confirmation, the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> was observed by SEM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>). The spherical morphology of the electrode is still preserved. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10a–e</a>, after the cycling tests, the carbon support frame is preserved, and the molybdenum compound is effectively confined inside the carbon sphere, which avoids the loss of active materials. The lattice spacing distance of ∼0.21 nm (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10f</a>) corresponds to the (141) crystal plane of the MoO<sub>3</sub> phase (JCPDS No. 05-0508). The SAED pattern contains the (141), (021), and (110) diffraction rings of the MoO<sub>3</sub> phase (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10g</a>). In the XRD pattern (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10h</a>), characteristic peaks belonging to MoO<sub>3</sub> can also be observed. EDS mapping (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10i</a>) shows the spatial distribution of the different elements. (2) <i>Enhanced rate capability</i>. The metallic-phase MoS<sub>2</sub> and the carbon substrate have good electrical conductivity, and the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>–HCS interface covalent interaction also facilitates the efficient electron transfer. Additionally, the larger interlayer spacing of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> material together with the mesoporous shell structure and hollow structure of HCS speed up the ion and mass transportation. (3) <i>Improved capacity</i>. The composite electrode possesses a high specific surface area and thus affords abundant active sites to store sodium ions. Taking together, the excellent sodium storage performance of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode can be ascribed to the structural design at the micro-/nanoscale.</div><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/medium/am1c18269_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0010.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. (a, b) SEM images of the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode after 100 cycles at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/asset/images/large/am1c18269_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c18269&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i16">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we demonstrate the controlled synthesis of novel ultrathin M-MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets on the inner surface of mesoporous HCS by a facile template-assisted solvothermal strategy. The composite electrode shows several unique structural characters, including the metallic-phase structure of the ultrathin MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets with a large interlayer spacing, the growth of M-MoS<sub>2</sub> on the inner surface of HCS via a strong covalent interaction, the flexible nature of the ultrathin M-MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets and the nanoscale hollow carbon spheres, and the large specific surface area. The resulting electrode exhibits outstanding sodium storage reversible capability and stability performance at various current densities. Specifically, the M-MoS<sub>2</sub>@HCS electrode delivers a reversible capacity of 291.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. After 100 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the reversible capacity is 401.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a capacity retention rate of 79%. After 2500 cycles at 1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the electrode still has a reversible capacity of 320.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a capacity retention ratio of 75%. The special structural design of the composite electrode is the cause of the improved electrochemical performance. It should be mentioned that by increasing the amount of reactants, it is possible to upscale the electrode production for possible industrial applications. The present strategy can also be used for the preparation of other novel electrodes based on transition-metal dichalcogenides for ion storage as well as energy conversion.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i17">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c18269?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c18269</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Further information on characterizations and electrochemical performance for various samples is available in the Supporting Information linked to this article. It contains SEM images, Raman and XRD patterns, and additional electrochemical characterizations and measurements (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c18269/suppl_file/am1c18269_si_001.pdf">am1c18269_si_001.pdf (2.05 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.1c18269" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Performance and cost of materials for lithium-based rechargeable automotive batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Schmuch, Richard; Wagner, Ralf; Hoerpel, Gerhard; Placke, Tobias; Winter, Martin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Energy</span>
